B006BAEQB2|4.0|The Kingmaker's Daughter tells the story of the War of the Roses from the perspective of Anne Neville, the shy daughter of Richard Neville, who was instrumental in putting Edward IV on the throne of England.  What is so intriguing is the uncertain feeling about Richard III the reader is left with. First, we see him through the eyes of a young Anne, completely devoted to him and the strong, but loving and tender side of Richard's personality is showcased.  Slowly, cracks in the armour of chivalry appear, and a darker side is shown.  The reader is left wondering why Richard left himself an out by not filing for the papal dispensation as he promised Anne before they married.  After their son's untimely death and Anne's subsequent physical and emotional disintegration, is he really courting the young Princess Elizabeth of York (or Mistress Elizabeth Grey, as Anne insists she be called), or merely putting on a show for Hunry Tudor's benefit?  I'll let you decide, but it seemed to me that he was ready to put Anne aside. He had lost his heir and Anne was unlikely to bear another child- this left him politically vulnerable.<br />In the end, Anne discovers that so much of her life was predicated on apparently false assumptions- Elizabeth Woodville wasn't really out to get her, her sister was probably not poisoned, her nephew likely died of SIDS- not as a result of the Queen's vindictive nature. Her years of hating- indeed, Anne herself gave the Queen control of her emotional well-being and very possibly misinterpreted many of Elizabeth's actions because of her assumptions and fear.Over the course of the story, Anne evolves from an amenable young lady to a suspicious- dare I say-hateful- woman.I have loved all of the books in this series and like seeing the events through the eyes of three different characters.  I can't wait for the next book- I believe Elizabeth of York is up to bat.

B008D4AKYQ|5.0|In my 5 figure CD collection , prog holds a very special place. And in that special place are a few bands that stand way above the rest in that genre. Big Big Train is one of those bands.<br />I have everything released by the band including the very rare \\"Bard\\" , but \\"English Electric Part One\\" is soon becoming a favorite. There simply isn't a wasted or dull moment on this disc.<br />The lyrics are thoughtful , the musicianship is exquisite , and the performance is stunning from second one to the very end.<br />I love how they tied the second track of the album into the last without seeming gimmicky. A reprise has been done billions of times , yet this one works due to it's subtlety and without fanfare.<br />I believe one of my favorite facets of this band is that they continually make progressive music that is not bombastic or overblown. Although that technique works for many a prog band , Big Big Train succeeds on a usage of quiet , understated passages that flow so seamlessly that you don't realize 8 or 12 minutes have gone by. The flute work is especially welcomed , and is in it's best usage since the Peter Gabriel era of Genesis.<br />I wish \\"English Electric Part Two\\" was coming out next week , and not next year!<br />Buy everything from Big Big Train. You won't be disappointed.

030758836X|5.0|Gillian Flynn's Gone Girl: A Review<br />By Autumn Heigle<br /><br /> If you are fascinated by psychological warfare, sexual deviancy, and unsolved homicide cases, you should add Gone Girl by Gillian Flynn to your book list. It was a satisfying vacation read. The story involves a married couple, Amy and Nick Dunne, who lose their jobs as New York writers due to the waning market for printed news. It is the tale of trust-fund only-daughter meets self-made man from the backwaters of the Mississippi.<br /><br /> But abandon all expectations for cheese-stuffed romance or glittering goth boys, because this relationship quickly sours into a depraved, desperate mess of a marriage. When the money is gone and the two are forced to retreat to a washed up, bankrupt town in Missouri, they begin to peel back layers of each other's facades, and discover who they really married.<br /><br /> Flynn was born in Kansas City, Missouri and grew up with an intense interest in books and films. She received her undergraduate degrees in English and journalism at the University of Kansas and later earned her master's degree in journalism from Northwestern University. She wrote for ten years at Entertainment Weekly magazine before ascending into the world of published authors.<br /><br /> Gone Girl is a New York Times best seller and Flynn's third novel. Her first novel, Sharp Objects, was released in 2006 and received two of Britain's Dagger awards. Her second novel, Dark Places, was Publishers Weekly Best Book of 2009 and Chicago Tribune Favorite Fiction choice. Gilles Paquet-Brenner (Sarah's Key) is to direct the movie.<br /><br /> The book is separated into two major parts. By the time I reached the second, I had accrued many theories about the turn in plot. But nothing prepared me for the rabbit hole I was about to fall down. I found no white rodents, only the dark corners of a psychopath's mind.<br /><br /> Flynn alternates between the husband and wife's voices chapter by chapter. Her insight into the male and female psyche is uncanny. The characters unique personalities evolve through the action as the pieces drift together. Be warned, they will lie to you. I found my opinions of the characters and their situations evolved with the turn of each page. I was ravenously hungry for the conclusion to their twisted tale.<br /><br /> Around the last hundred pages, I was unable to set the book down. Huddled in the sink below the tiny window in a Parisian flat, I read by morning light to avoid disturbing my roommate. She awoke when I slammed the book shut and released an uncontainable, \\"Damn!\\" I was intrigued, angry, amused, and unsettled all at once.<br /><br /> If you are looking for a pink ribbon-wrapped ending sealed in grandma's kisses, stay the hell away from this book. But if you believe that the best stories are the ones that stir you to feel something new, perhaps uncomfortable, and leave you asking just a few questions, then by all means, pick it up.

042524489X|5.0|This week, we'll be looking at Kiss of Snow, one of the most memorable, and most anticipated installment of Nalini Singh's Psy-Changeling series.<br /><br />The romantic pairing that this book features has been hinted at in several book throughout the series, and the story was highly anticipated by many fans. It was absolutely worth the wait! Hawke and Sienna's story is memorable, and worthy of the character's strong personalities.<br /><br />Hawke is the Alpha of Snowdancer, the most powerful changeling wolf pack in the United States. He became Alpha at a young age when a tragedy wiped out most of the elders in the pack. Sienna is a young Psy woman who, with her family, became an adopted member of Snowdancer in her youth. She holds the rank of novice soldier within the pack. Sienna's situation is unusual. The training she had in her intensely traumatic childhood, paired with constant struggles to manage her destructive Psy power, have strengthened and matured her at an accelerated rate. She often seems older than her true age, but in some things she is very much the inexperienced young girl. There seems to be a huge gap separating the characters, including differences in rank, age, background, and experience. Then there is an added complication: the girl Hawke knew would grow up to be his mate died in a childhood accident, so it is unlikely that Hawke and Sienna will ever formally mate, even if they manage to overcome all of those differences. When you add in the external conflicts that come from the further development of the over-arching series plotline, you are left with a rich, complex series of plots that all have to be fully developed and woven together cohesively-- I believe that the author managed it beautifully.<br />Throughout the series, Singh has done a remarkable job constructing a believable alternate culture, and the world building is some of the best I have ever seen. The alternate culture and world are constructed through seamless integration withthe story in each book, avoiding the bulky chunks of information dump that are so often seen in scifi-fantasy, and paranormal novels. Kiss of Snow continues in the tradition of its predecessors, further building and enriching the world through the natural progress of the story. The elaborate over-arching plot of the series progresses nicely in this installment, significant events taking place, and influencing the behavior of Sienna and Hawke.<br /><br />One of the memorable features of the Psy-Changeling series is a theme emphasizing the interconnectedness, and interdependence, of life; the three major races in the world (the Psy, the Changelings, and the Humans) are connected in a myriad of ways, even if they don't realize it, and the major conflicts usually arise from a failure to acknowledge this interconnectedness. In addition, the deliberateness of nature, and the importance of balance is another prevalent theme. Psy powers are the most obvious example of this. Powers that are seemingly completely benevolent contain a potential for darkness, and powers that appear to have no purpose other than absolute violence contain the potential for powerful good.<br /><br />Sienna's power is one of the most destructive. Up until the end of the story, there seems to be no way it can be controlled, or channeled into anything other than absolute destruction; however, when the true function of her power is revealed, the subtly placed hints throughout the story come together beautifully, a web of fine lace finally settling into a satisfying completeness.<br /><br />The main plot, Sienna and Hawke's love story, is complex. They are powerfully drawn to each other, but they must bridge internal obstacles of power, age, status, and emotional maturity, as well as several external issues: hostile political climate, uncertain future, time constraints due to preparation for war, etc. Also, both characters have deep-rooted fears and past trauma that has affected their development as individuals, and affects the development of their relationship. As the story progresses, the reader sees both characters slowly grow and mature to a place where a functional relationship is possible. In the end, both Hawke and Sienna learn how to forge an equal partnership, despite their differences. Overall, Kiss of Snow was powerful thematically, well-paced and exciting in the overarching series plotline, and emotionally engaging, finely nuanced, and well-structured in the main romantic plotline. The balance between the romantic plotline and the secondary plotline was spot on as well. The grad external conflict of the world was further developed- the events of this book formed a major climax in the series plotline--and a significant part of the story, without overwhelming the romantic plotline, which remained the main focus (exactly as it should in a paranormal romance).<br /><br />Kiss of Snow was another wonderful example of world building, multi-plot management, and character and relationship development from Nalini Singh.<br /><br />This review was written by me, and originally posted on [...]

I DO like his \\"less horror-ible\\" tales best -- The Stand is an epic that bears close comparison with The Lord of The Rings trilogy (THINK about it), and he earned my whole-hearted respect when he turned out \\"Gerald's Game\\" -- a thriller-chiller without a monster in it (Okay, one -- but at the very end, and there was a good explanation) and Deolores Claiborne.  In both these novels, King got inside a woman's head and presented two stories of dilemma (one physical & one introspective) with an uncannily spot-one feminine point of view that in some passages actually made me cry.<br /><br />I was an adult woman when Kennedy was assassinated, and in the ensuing years have wondered what he might have accomplished and how he would have influenced our country's future if Lee Harvey Oswald had missed his first shot.  This book didn't answer that question -- but of course it couldn't -- because none of us have the faintest idea, and not even Stephen King would dare to speculate on a U.S. born of a two-term Kennedy presidency.  So THAT didn't disappoint me.<br /><br />I WAS disappointed, however (not in a devastating fashion that made me throw the book down in disgust, but disappointed none-the-less).  I was disappointed because I really didn't care about any of the characters -- I really didn't think the premise was  believable at any level -- and most of all, Stevie, you screwed up a fact or two along the way.  I even checked them out, just to make sure that my own ancient mind had it right.  Those little glitches interfered with my enjoyment of this book -- they made me say \\"I don't THINK so, Stephen,\\" and sent me to my Google machine.  And this was sad, because inall the years I've been reading Stephen King books I have NEVER questioned the veracity of the details.<br /><br />Alas, we are ALL aging -- even Stephen King.  And I STILL believe that he is without peer in today's writing community (he's the best of the 20th Century writers, in my book -- and I don't care if he did write mostly \\"horror\\" stories).  But this book didn't cut it for me (although I DID read it from cover-to-cover).<br /><br />I won't tell you the mistakes in the book (but I DID write Stephen a letter pointing them out) -- if you didn't grow up in the 50's & 60's you'll never spot them, and in spite of all that, this WAS still a good read.

B008MG1E4A|5.0|The book consists actually of two books of about the same size. The first one after a teaser from Abbottabad goes way back in time, even to the first author's childhood and presents somewhat interesting stuff not specific to the Obama raid.<br /><br />The second half, however, writes too little about some details of the raid. Since the facts have been generally known for some time, I hope I am not spoiling much by mentioning a few issues here.<br /><br />I may have to re-read part of the book again but it does not bother to explain why the first helicopter went down. Not by hostile fire, as far as I can tell. Are these infernal machines so unreliable? It is not a job of a SEAL like the author to understand the specifics but, after all, he could have lost his life right there, just like the poor guys ordered to rescue hostages years ago under Carter. The seasoned pilot made the right decisions at the critical moment and no one got hurt and a modified plan was immediately implemented.<br /><br />I hesitate to mention the 'controversy' of apparent killing Bin Laden at the moment when he was not an immediate threat to the Americans. To me this fact is of no significance at all but the New York Times immediately wrote about this 'legal' aspect of the raid. Does anyone believe that Ben Laden would have surrendered peacefully if politely asked to do so? Not this reader.<br /><br />It is clear from the context that the author(s) are not Obama admirers. Their comments, however, are quite fair and bit bitter - Obama seemed to promise them couple of beers in the White House and it never happened. They guys deserve the highest honors this country can award them but a superb intelligence by the CIA (for a change from the Tenet years) has been duly acknowledged by the author - perhaps by a composite person identified as a female CIA analyst.<br /><br />As for the 'secrets' revealed in the book, there may be something to it. Not any specific secrets but people who mean us harm cansave some time mimicking our commando operations going through parts of the book that show the 'spirit' and general approach to the tasks at hand when you run this kind of operation.<br /><br />To me, this is a price to be paid to keep us Americans up to date on the state of our most advanced military units of the 21st century. Not only we spend a lot of money on the military but the author makes it possible to relate to them as completely dedicated human beings with their flaws under a very good control. I found one issue bit disturbing: the heavy dependence on the sleeping pill Ambien mentioned many time in the book. It is probably essential for people on 'vampire' shifts (not only commandos) but this is, as far as I know, an addictive stuff.<br /><br />As an agnostic I appreciate the  virtual absence of any religious references in the book. Both ways: no American military chaplains sending the commando with his prayers and blessings, no redundant mentioning of the Islam fanaticism except for reporting the factual Allahu Akbar shouted by the insurgents in Iraq in the first (non-Osama) part of the book. That's how it should be and not only in the elite military units.<br /><br />On a more general level, our enemies should realize that we can afford a luxury like this very expensive (just the IR goggles cost about $65K a piece) raids only very rarely.<br /><br />More typical are the drone attacks and they simply cannot protect the families of the terrorists like the men of our commandos usually do.<br /><br />Collateral damage by the drones is completely unavoidable while, as the book reports, a major care was taken in this case not to protect the 'innocent' (the women and children are actually facilitators to their murderous husbands and fathers) but not necessarily armed and fighting directly at the moment. The author acknowledges, being a really experienced fighter, that suicidal women and perhaps even small children (everyone can wear an exploding garment) wereon their minds while they avoided to harm most of the them.<br /><br />It is certainly more efficient to send over a drone and be done with it.<br /><br />This is my longest review in these places but this has been, so far, the most important book I have ever bought for my Kindle.<br /><br />A few words on 9/8/12. Just out of curiosity I used Bing (they have nice opening pages) and looked for the reasons for the helo crash that almost killed the author.<br /><br />ABSOLUTELY NOTHING but idiotic speculations not worth even looking at. No wonder the author did not want to get into it. Ground effects, some wise guys are saying. Who knows? 'Owen' just limited his story to what he really knew and what his job was over there. No nonsense guy, perhaps a hero. And a pretty good ghost writer as well. I can just see these two guys with the SEAL describing the facts and the other guy recording his words or directly typing them on something - there are many choices available now for doing so.<br /><br />Both did a great job. The author promises do donate most of the proceeds to the families of his 'brothers' who did not make it. There can be hardly a more admirable goal.<br /><br />I actually changed my rating to all five stars.

0525953728|5.0|This was a very easy read.  Got it in the mail last night, started on it in bed, finished it this morning.  Maybe 4 - 5 hours.  What I wasn't expecting was very little politics in it.  After all of the talk, I was expecting this to be smeared with thousands of words of anti-Obama slogans.<br /><br />Instead, what you get is a 10 year walk through the life of a Navy SEAL turned DEVGRU Operator.  Yes, he distinguishes them differently in the book, and by the end you get a much better feel for that hierarchy.  It begins with some talk of his early life in Alaska and how the harsh realities of being a modern day pioneer helped prepare him for his days as a future war fighter.<br /><br />Most of the 300 or so pages are dedicated to his life training for DEVGRU and the missions after qualifying for that team.  He takes you through several (of the dozen or so) deployments in and out of Afghanistan and Iraq, setting up the culture of DEVGRU.  In that time, the reader is introduced to the many mistakes and serendipitous events that shaped DEVGRU and the author's tactics during the War on Terror.<br /><br />The human side is not at all what one would expect.  No Easy Day goes out of its way to paint the Operators, as they are called, as normal working class husbands, albeit absentee husbands.<br /><br />The final 20% of the book is dedicated to the Osama bin Laden raid preparation, execution, and aftermath.  If you were looking to get only that in exquisite detail, you will be disappointed.  The details are there more so than they have been in the media, but you never are given access to anything that should remain \\"Top Secret\\".  The fact that this book has received so much attention lately for that purpose is surprising to me, as everything in No Easy Day can be found by piecing together other sources.  They just happen to be consolidated and actually verified by an eye witness in No Easy Day.<br /><br />If you were hoping for an indictment of Obama, you will not find it inthis book.  Yes, there are a few lines that mention President Obama and some of it is positive while some of it is negative.  However, most of the political elements are aimed at the process of decision making in Washington (not so much the decision makers), the leaks that came after the raid (the author never directs blame at any one person for this), and most importantly the fact that all politicians take more credit than they deserve for military operations gone well.<br /><br />The most enjoyable part of No Easy Day for me was learning more about the people behind-the-scenes.  There were a multitude of intelligence analysts, CIA personnel, pilots, DEVGRU command and trainers, and even the interpreter that made Operation Neptune Spear not only a smashing success, but also one without any casualties on the American side.  These men and women are finally given the credit they deserve by one of the men who actually fired the rounds in Abbottobad.

We read of the many trials and tribulations she endures and through her story, we see the incredible power of human emotions, compassion, altruism, and love in its many forms. The portrayal of the residents of a German town during the second world war shows us that German people were no different to others around the world and that although there were many who supported Hitler and his appalling reign, there were others who risked their lives to help Jewish people. The structure of the book is also unusual but again refreshing. The vocabulary used in the book makes it very easy to read but it is still powerfully emotive. Best of Metallica, best of Metal?<br /><br />Cons: Nothing.<br /><br />Master of Puppets stands today as what I, and many others, feel to be the crown jewel in the Metallica catalog. Sure it's tough to really decide between the first four albums, but I feel this was the perfect mesh of their thrash metal tinged with the \\"true\\" musical elements that had become the bands' hallmark.<br /><br />It was released in 1986 - As Metallica's third album - and would sadly be the last with bassist Cliff Burton - who would be killed in a tragic tour bus accident. And while Burton was not the primary song writer, and Jason Newsted has filled in admirably, it was clear the Metallica would never be the same with Burton.<br /><br />This release is from a less radio friendly era (Before Metal got much radio/MTV airplay). There are only 8 songs, but none of them clock in at less than 5 minutes. The songs are powerful - they run the gambit of emotions, the musical variety is astounding. These are not merely songs - but compositions - as if done by a classical master. Delves into the darkness with \\"The Thing that Should Not Be\\" - Which is really a song that I didn't care for much at first, but the lyrical mastery and the subtlety of the song have slowly grown on me over the years, and truly is an amazing song.<br /><br />Of course, the favorite of the repressed - \\"Welcome Home (Sanitarium)\\" - Is viewed by many as the best song of its kind - mixing the best of metal and rock ballad as well - as well as pertinent, serious subject matter - Into a masterpiece of a tune.<br /><br />\\"Disposable Heroes\\" - The anti-military song follows, and is fast and hard , and angry, much like \\"Battery\\". \\"Leper Messiah\\" - A slow rock tune that was probably the basis for their later song \\"Sad but True\\", musically anyway. In the great trend of instrumentals from the band comes - \\"Orion\\" - Which, while I don't feel is quite as good as \\"Call of Cthulu\\" off of Ride the Lightning, is still an amazing song - and really showcases the bands musical talent.<br /><br />And to wrap things up \\"Damage Inc.\\" - which really brings together all the elements of the album. Musically, lyrically, and thematicly (The album in general is about people in power) into one cataclysmic finale. No, it's not the best song on the disc - But probably is a great mish-mash of everything else you'll find on it - and still a great song.<br /><br />So try to put aside your current feelings about Metallica and whatever controversy they may be involved in. When you're talking about Master of Puppets - You could be talking about the best metal release of all time. And an entire generation of metal heads have to give it some credit for their influence.<br /><br />(NOTE: This review was originally written 12/8/00)

B000002H6C|4.0|Pros:Wonderful Musicianship, a few Metallica classics.<br /><br />Cons:Flat production, some overdrawn songs.<br /><br />The Bottom Line: The last of the Great Metallica albums, all metal fans should own it.<br /><br />Quick Facts:<br />Metallica - And Justice For All. Released 1988, Heavy Metal genre. Contains \\"hit\\" song - One. 9 total songs, 65 minutes total album time.<br /><br />Full Review:<br /><br />1988 was in many ways, the end of the 80's Heavy Metal sound, but it was a great fanfare. Danzig released his first solo album, Iron Maiden's Seventh Son was released - Anthrax put out State of Euphoria. In late 87, Guns and Roses released the classic Appetite. 1988 was really right before the explosion of industrial metal and alternative rock that was to follow. It was an end of an era for Metallica as well, in several ways. Metallica distinctly changed their sound after the release of And Justice, finding many of the songs simply to complicated to perform live. The formula that they had used for three straight albums (Ride The Lightning, Master of Puppets, and Justice) had worn a bit thin, and the band would go in a new direction with the \\"Black Album\\".<br /><br />What Justice became then, was really a swan song of sorts, not just for Metallica's \\"old\\" sound, but for it would be a gravestone for almost an entire sound of music. Metallica's most complex and ambitious album, And Justice For All is both loved and hated by many Metallica fans. Some find it to be too much, others view it as a great album with a few quirks.<br /><br />Something immidiately noticible is the production values. The snare drum pops out as a tad to sharp, the bass is quiet. The focus here is clearly on the guitars, which while very good, unfortunately takes some flavor out of the overall sound - which some have described as flat. Whether it was done to further the theme of the album (The ever decaying society), is uknown - but you can't help but hear it in the album.<br /><br />This albumwill also be known for the song that put Metallica toward the mainstream, with \\"One\\" - which became a classic heavy metal hit, and got tons of radio and MTV airplay, and really introduced the band to many people who didn't catch Headbanger's Ball. While One is viewed as one of Metallica's best songs by many, this album contains a few of the bands finest works. Unfortunately, a few of the songs end up a tad bland and overdrawn - No song on the album is shorter than 5:12, and 7 of them are longer than 6 minutes. While it makes the good songs enjoyable to listen to, it makes the poorer ones tend to drag on.<br /><br />Here's the track listing (Favorite cuts are *'d)<br /><br />1.* Blackened<br />2. And Justice For All<br />3.* Eye Of The Beholder<br />4.* One<br />5. Shortest Straw, The<br />6. Harvester Of Sorrow<br />7.* Frayed Ends Of Sanity, The<br />8. To Live Is To Die<br />9. Dyers Eve<br /><br />Blackened starts off the album, and is not only my favorite song on the album, but one of my favorite Metallica songs of all time. It starts of slow, then kicks in with a Battery-like power. Great vocals, great lyrics - and just a total power song. One of Metallica's finest.<br /><br />And Justice for All is a decent tune, though at 9:44, is a bit too long and gets a bit repetitive. It has a bit of everything Metallica has to offer, but it doesn't excel at any of them.<br /><br />Eye of the Beholder doesn't get a lot of kudos from most Met fans, but it really stands as my 2nd favorite tune on the release. The intro alone gets you rocking out, and then the song just continues with incredible guitar licks and a sweet chorus. I like the lyrics a lot too.<br /><br />One - Most people are familiar with this one, there's not a ton I am going to say about it. I like the song, but I don't love it.<br /><br />The Shortest Straw and Harvester of Sorrow both sound very similar to me - both are fair, and the flat production really shows on both of them. They feel like \\"going through the motions\\" songs, a tad to repeitious with nothing extraordinary to offer.<br /><br />The Frayed Ends of Sanity stands out because of the interesting tempo the song uses, as well as the chanting introduction. Maybe it is that intro that gets you singing along, but this song really gets me into it - Some great early guitar work - and a solid song all the way through.<br /><br />To Live is to Die is the instrumental offering on this album, co-written by Bassist Cluff Burton who had died on tour for the Master of Puppets album. It's actually quite musical, and very long - 9:48. But it doesn't stand out as being better than Call of Cthulu off of Ride the Lightning or Orion of of Master of Puppets, so you'll never say - \\"Man I gotta hear To live is to die!\\"<br /><br />Dyers Eve finishes off the album, and is the most thrash inspired track on the album. The best part of the song is the lyrics, but the music - while fast and furious, doesn't have enough direction or good riffs to keep one interested.<br /><br />Do you need what I need? Boundries overthrown, Look inside, to each his own!\\" (Eye of the Beholder)<br /><br />What I liked:<br /><br />Ambitious, musical album.<br /><br />Some of the best songs written by the band.<br /><br />Blackened kicks you in the teeth.<br /><br />What I didn't like:<br /><br />Production makes album a tad flat.<br /><br />Some of the songs are too long.<br /><br />A few of the songs are very bland.<br /><br />Overall:<br /><br />This is the last of the great Metallica albums, and the end of an era. There are several great songs that make this a \\"must have\\" album, but it's not non-stop greatness the way Ride the Lightning and Master of Puppets were before it. It belongs in any Metal fans collection.<br /><br />(NOTE: This review was written 4/3/04)

She saw nearly her entire family taken away and slain. It was only Kevin's intervention that kept her from dying in the US from the many illneseses she took on during the war years. So she is dearly devoted to him, even through his manic highs and violent lows.<br /><br />Peter comes along as a barely-of-age writer from the South who wants to write the next American novel in the streets of Brooklyn. The three form a friendship, and over time Peter is able to learn more and more about what Meryl went through.<br /><br />Have I mentioned Meryl is an incredible actress? Her accent is pure music on the ears. She's shown in other movies that it's not just this Polish accent that she excels at, but she can do a wide range of characters.<br /><br />It's stunning that there's even scenes of females talking with other females, which you pretty much NEVER get in any movie. Usually it's all about men with perhaps a woman or two sprinkled in for entertainment. B0066DNZCA|3.0|After reading and enjoying Hill's ghostly novel THE WOMAN IN BLACK, I went looking for other spooky tales she might've crafted and found this one right away (thanks to its subtitle, \\"A Ghost Story\\").  This novella is about an evil painting, but not in the way of THE PICTURE OF DORIAN GRAY (or AMITYVILLE: THE NEW GENERATION, for that matter, though technically that was a mirror, not a painting).<br /><br />It begins with a young man named Oliver visiting his elderly mentor, Theo Parmitter, who is ailing and close to death after years of being a revered but lonely scholar.  The two begin to talk and as Oliver studies Theo's apartment, a painting along the back wall catches his eye.  He goes to look at it more closely, and finds there an 18th century scene of a carnival on the streets of Venice.  It's very detailed work, and obviously old.  Only, the more Oliver looks at it, the more he becomes somewhat troubled by certain elements.  First, it seems every one of the people painted in it is looking right at him, even as he moves from side to side, and then Oliver notices that one of the men, who is depicted looking terrified as he's being carted off by two others, seems to look anachronistic.  Wrong clothes, strange facial expression, something just a little off.<br /><br />When Oliver mentions the painting to Theo, Theo decides it's time to tell someone the true story of the piece, so he sits Oliver down and begins.  After telling the first part of the story, which is primarily about how Theo came to own the painting, Theo is so exhausted he begs Oliver to let him finish the tale another day.  Frustrated and wanting to know more, Oliver reluctantly agrees and then is horrified to discover Theo nearly dies of a stroke later that evening.  Blaming himself for pushing Theo to tell him a story that was clearly upsetting, Oliver decides to stay in town and help him get back on his feet.  When Theo's feeling better, he finally tells Oliver the rest of the truth about the painting, a story that involves an evil woman and the disappearances of several people who later appear in the painting as if by magic.  Or, more accurately, by curse.<br /><br />It's not a terribly scary story, all in all -- in fact, it's kind of hokey.  But the same was true of THE WOMAN IN BLACK, and, as with both books, the reason I ended up really enjoying them is because Hill is extremely talented at setting a scene.  Her descriptions of both characters and the spaces they inhabit are subtle perfection.  She never spends paragraphs simply describing things -- instead, you pick up on things slowly, forming a detailed image in your mind as you read.  The way the characters talk, for one thing, plays a major role in how they start to look in your mind.  That's crafty writing, right there.  Same thing goes for physical sensations the characters report -- the smell of a room, the touch of an object.  She's very, very good at that sort of thing, which goes a long way toward letting me overlook some flaws in her storytelling.

B001152TL6|2.0|Our culture is so based on derivation and copy-catting that any artist who stands on his own two feet, masters his medium, and speaks to our heart with an authentic voice, even once, achieves something like cult status. This means that he receives the corrolaries of our unbalanced appreciation of his magic art: fanboyism and effusive appreciation. Essentially, his fans can begin to see his art as a perceptual bench press machine on which to exercise their powers of appreciation. There is very little the human mind cannot appreciate or find meaning in, when the intention is set to do so. There are rocks and trees which the Buddha would have given 5 stars, if you get my meaning.<br /><br />Lynch's Lost Highway disregards narrative structure and typical plotline progression, takes an oblique/surrealist approach to character development, and leaves the viewer mostly in the dark about what is happening through most of the film. The pacing is slow enough to allow the viewer to truly enjoy the delicious, almost unequalled visuals. The visuals are so stunning, that in spite of everything, I sometime watch bits of this film to enjoy them again. However the enjoyment of the visuals is such that it often leads the viewer to wonder: does this film have a larger meaning outside of its own aesthetic beauty?<br /><br />The viewer is given an intimate experience of stunning, sensuous imagery, but not one with any coherent movement or meaning. The dissonance coded into the film's progression is deliberately jarring and is effectively suspenseful, but in a bad, bad way. It is building a monotonic, unresolved, almost sadistic suspense which happens at the expense of the viewer's enjoyment. A chord progression that ceaselessly emphasizes the ii/vii/minor-7th chords is not going to give a pleasant resolution, and carried on for longer than 2 minutes would just be tedious beyond imagining. Yet Lynch isn't afraid to go into that territory plot-wise with this unending mystery-within-a-mystery shell game.<br /><br />Not enough love in this piece to make me forget that I'm looking at Art.

0547564651|2.0|Author Tough asserts that we overstress pupil achievement and under-emphasize skills like perseverance, curiosity, self-control, and optimism - a postulate that strikes me as inane on its face, and another attempt by educators seeking to avoid accountability for poor academic results.<br /><br />The past four decades or more of public education have been filled with one fad after another - each announced with positive fanfare and smiles, and often backed by seemingly credible research. Unfortunately, despite all these supposedly positive reports, augmented by more than tripling inflation-adjusted per-pupil expenditures, U.S. pupil achievement has improved very little, if at all - measured by 17-year-old NAEP test results and dropout rate data. (Assessing pupil performance trends over the years has been made unnecessarily complicated by frequent changes in the tests used, as well as a series of new definitions of 'high-school dropout.')<br /><br />Why the disappointing overall results? Reality is that the field of education research has been dominated by poor research methodology - uncontrolled studies, small sample sizes, 'after-the-fact 'data-mining,' attrition of initial gains, and failure to replicate being the most prevalent causes.<br /><br />Author Tough continues in that unfortunate tradition, beginning with after-the-fact data mining of the Perry Preschool Project. The Perry Preschool project was a 1962 intensive preschool program for disadvantaged youth intended to boost academic achievement. Initial results were positive, but subsequently faded to the point that the program was judged a failure by those most objective. Subsequent 'digging' years later 'revealed,' however, that those in the program did far better in life outcome measures such as incarceration, welfare status, high-school graduation, and employment at age 27. Unfortunately, any competent statistics teacher will strongly suggest the need for additional confirmation since the original research was not designed to detect such differences. Regardless, Tough fails to report the sizes of the newly-found benefits - very poor reporting guaranteed to produce unwarranted conclusions. (The actual differences were operationally significant - lucky for Mr. Tough; however, I didn't have the time or interest in checking on the rest of his reporting.)<br /><br />Other anecdotal sources - Geoffrey Canada's social service organization in Harlem, and stories of individual teenagers in Chicago and elsewhere. More useful material comes from psychologist Mischel's studies from the 1960s reporting that children able to resist immediately eating a marshmallow in return for two marshmallows later did better in school and were more successful as adults.<br /><br />Another repetitive problem with books summarizing research findings - they often omit findings that contradict the desired conclusion. Unless one is an expert, it is very difficult to tell without doing one's own exhaustive review of the literature. Since the nature of social sciences is that contradictory findings are often found in attempted replications, mega-analyses by reputable, qualified statisticians has become the 'gold standard.' Author Tough doesn't even begin to approach that.<br /><br />Finally, the most useful analyses take Occam's Razor into any explanation of findings. Here again, Mr. Tough fails - he has his conclusion and he's going to prove it, no matter how complicated and hard to follow his logic becomes. The simplest explanation of what he found is that youngsters growing up without positive role models are unlikely to succeed in later life. This relationship is about as obvious as the fact that the sun rises in the East.<br /><br />Finally, to be fair, author Tough has a few nuggets of truth embedded in his long-winded, convoluted story. The correlation between both IQ and academic achievement vs. adult successes (eg. earnings, position) is far less than one would think. However, perseverance etc. w/o learning skills/achievement isn't likely to be very successful either. The Asian/Confucian model combines both - education is held in high regard, and perserverence towards academic achievement is strongly encouraged by high-stakes testing that determines college admissions. Collectively, its graduates (nations) are doing very, very well. In other words, 'culture matters' - the exact opposite of an earlier education fad claiming that all value sets were equal. And that conclusion is much more easily validated, and already been done repeatedly involving many millions over decades and across nations by sources far more credible than Mr. Tough.c. w/o learning skills/achievement isn't likely to be very successful either. 1781002231|5.0|[[VIDEOID:mo1HA650F41GLQA]]<br /><br />OF TRADE AND WTO STANDARDS OF REVIEW<br /><br />An appreciation by Phillip Taylor MBE and Elizabeth Taylor of Richmond Green Chambers<br /><br />This latest book from Edward Elgar Publishing concerns more or less obliquely, the sometimes uneasy relationship between the WTO (World Trade Organisation) and its member states.  The author Ross Becroft, both a lawyer and academic, is a specialist in this area and has delivered a thorough critique of the workings of WTO review panels, the purpose of which is to review whether a WTO member state has complied with its obligations under the WTO agreements.<br /><br />Compliance appears to be the operative word here, another key issue being deference and the degree of deference that WTO panels should apply to measures enacted by WTO members; environmental measures, for example.  The WTO, it would appear, needs to aim for a balance between deferring too much -- or not enough -- as the case may be, to the wishes and whims of individual member states.<br /><br />Becroft therefore calls for a changed and more flexible standard of review that would correspondingly vary according to the type of measure, or policy under examination, i.e. in the author's words, `a non-deferential standard of review when examining domestic measures'. He concludes that `this new standard of review would significantly enhance the WTO dispute settlement system, thereby developing and strengthening the WTO and the international trading system'.<br /><br />For international lawyers and scholars in the field of international relations and economic law, including those with a special interest in the workings of the WTO and international organizations generally, this book is quite a find.<br /><br />Andrew Stoler -- formerly the Deputy Director General of the WTO and Executive Director, Institute for International Trade -- has called this `an important book' which, 'should be considered to be on the required reading list of anyone professionally involved in dispute settlement at the WTO'.<br /><br />Within its 250 or so pages, this excellent monograph encapsulates in a single volume at least eight years of the author's research.  If you are doing research of your own in this wide ranging area, this book's fourteen page bibliography alone will prove absolutely invaluable. Also, there are extensive tables of cases, legislation, plus a useful list of abbreviations and a detailed index at the back.  The publication date is 2012.of anyone professionally involved in dispute settlement at the WTO'.<br /><br />Within its 250 or so pages, this excellent monograph encapsulates in a single volume at least eight years of the author's research. 0375842209|5.0|What can I say about The Book Thief? Hmm... The Book Thief. Well the truth is I can say a lot of things about The Book Thief because this novel is the most beautifully written but tragic, most heart warming but provoking, kind but beyond cruel book I have ever read. And that's why I love it. I must say I love sad books; ones that really strip you down so you become just a puddle of tears on the floor. It really brings an answer to why I have read Anne Frank, The Boy in the Striped Pijamas, Private Peaceful, Unbroken etc. but The Book Thief is beyond my favorite.<br /><br />First off I love the language in this novel. Markus Zusak is a beautiful writer who told his story through Death. He took his words which contained so much intricacy, quirk but depth and planted them into the soul of such a prominent character in the times of WW2. Zusak allows such detail and personality into Deaths words that no human could achieve. I found that the second time I read this book - I knew the story-  I put all my focus into the words. It took me double the time it took to first read the book because of how much thought you can put into each point. Every word seems to have two meanings which are impossible to ignore. So you grasp each word and hold on for the journey.<br /><br />Secondly, the story of the novel is so dark and cruel it sounds like Liesel (the main character) is in a survival story. It's not that she's sick- she was abandoned, her brother died and she never knew her father. Who could take that emotional roller coaster? Well the truth is her story get's worse before it gets better. But, before I turn you off this book, I'd like you to know it's the brief moments in the middle- whether Rudy is asking for a kiss or Liesel is stealing a book from the Mayor's house- you can't help but laugh at these happy moments but as Death tells you it won't last long...<br /><br />Lastly, this book to me was very real. Anything that happened in this book either was real or could have been. And that's what brought the most sting in the end: Lot's of people suffered just like Liesel. Many people were starving in their homes just like Liesel, Many people had loved ones die just like Liesel but what many people didn't have was the hope Liesel had and that's what made me love this book the most.

0140186409|5.0|Sure, this novel is beautifully written, but the propaganda aspect of the work gets in the way pretty quickly. Every aspect of capitalism is condemned as evil by Steinbeck and the characters in the book are depicted as one of three types of people: A) The Victims, B) The Helpless Tools, C) The Evil Capitalists.<br /><br />The victims, such as the Joads, are portrayed as helpless one-trick ponies who, despite having some wonderful skills that could have made them some money, can't seem to adjust to the changing times and are either too dumb or too nostalgic to strike out on their own. They spend the entire novel drifting toward some sort of idiotic fantasy world that they're warned off of time and time again. Do they listen to the warnings? No. They continue stubbornly, in a way that isn't really explained by Steinbeck. Why exactly is it that the Joads don't believe the various characters telling them not to continue on to California? Who knows? Apparently, Steinbeck doesn't because he offers no explanation for -- or even acknowledgement of -- this.<br /><br />The Helpless Tools are those who serve the monsters, the corporations, which have grown uncontrollable by the men who created them. The tools do the bidding of the monstrous companies and, though they empathize with the Victims, they're helpless to resist the will of the companies -- the companies who eat profits and die without them.<br /><br />Then we encounter the Evil Capitalists. These are the evil men who actually dare to make a living for themselves by making a profit. Oh, stop! What are you!? The Devil!!?!? Good, God, man. Steinbeck would have fit right in at any of the recent 'Occupy' rallies anywhere in the world. Were he alive today, I'm sure he'd be giving some magnificent speech at the Democratic National Convention -- and hosted by Hugo Chavez soon after.<br /><br />Despite all of this though -- if you're a writer, this book is mandatory reading. There is so much masterful technique to learn from this book that you're only doing yourself a disservice by not reading it. You're literally limiting your potential because if you have the education and experience to appreciate it, this book is a gold mine of advanced technique.<br /><br />Pinch your nose at the politics and read this if you're a writer -- or else I'm giving you an automatic 'C'! :)this book that you're only doing yourself a disservice by not reading it. You're literally limiting your potential because if you have the education and experience to appreciate it, this book is a gold mine of advanced technique.<br /><br />Pinch your nose at the politics and read this if you're a writer -- or else I'm giving you an automatic 'C'! :)

B0079XPUOW|5.0|With love and kisses to Star Trek and Philip K. Dick, Scalzi delivers a great and challenging read with Redshirts.  It screwed with my mind and I loved it.<br /><br />For the people who say they read Old Man's War and don't get this book, you don't know Scalzi.  This follows more in the tradition of Agent to the Stars and Android's Dream.  It mixes crazy \\"what if\\" science fiction with a healthy dose of humor.  And, deep questions do surface, including what makes good TV writing, what does free will mean (and are you really happy if you have it), and the nature of love and fate.<br /><br />Scalzi deconstructs the some of the absurd cliches original Star Trek series.  Why is Kirk always safe on away missions, but the red shirted security guy always dies?  If the show were actually happening to real people, how would the rest of the crew react when they were picked for an away mission, knowing what happens to their colleagues?  Wouldn't they know their chances weren't good?  For fans of the genre, there are plenty of laugh out loud moments.<br /><br />Scalzi repeatedly tramples the fourth wall, and the metafictional implications abound.  The characters grapple with metaphysical questions like this a space opera, or a show about a space opera, or a book about a show about a space opera?  Who is the god of this machine?  Who are the real main characters, and who are bit players?  Is Jimmy Hanson a MacGuffin or a shadow of the puppeteer?<br /><br />When I read negative reviews of this book, I just can't help but sadly think that people haven't understood it.  Perhaps it is too complex and smart for the mass audience.  Or perhaps the audience doesn't appreciate the \\"reality\\" of the book being screwed with so much.  Maybe the audience really does just want a simple narrative, where the hero overcomes adversity and everyone lives happily ever after.  No deep thinking required.<br /><br />But for those that like to think and are willing to follow Scalzi down this black hole,Redshirts really delivers.  Take it lightly, and marvel in its absurdity and depth.  But hey, maybe I'm really a sock puppet for the author and am just writing this review to pump the book.  As Scalzi says in Red Shirts, \\"No, no I'm just f...ing with you.\\"

0374203032|4.0|XXXXX<br /><br />\\"We live at a time when almost everything can be bought and sold.  Over the past three decades, markets--and market values--have come to govern our lives as never before.  We did not arrive at this condition through any deliberate choice.  It is almost as if it came upon us...<br /><br />Today, the logic of buying and selling no longer applies to material goods alone but increasingly governs the whole of life.  It is time to ask whether we want to live this way...<br /><br />We [also] need to ask whether there are some things money should not buy.  [In other words, what are the moral limits of markets?]\\"<br /><br />The above comes from the introduction of this very interesting book by Michael J. Sandel.  He is a Professor of Government at Harvard University.  Sandel is also an author.  His writings have been translated into 18 languages, and his lectures on Justice have been viewed, online and on T.V., throughout the world.<br /><br />In an economic or business transaction of any kind, both seller and buyer, it is claimed, get what they want.  But are there moral implications to some of these transactions?<br /><br />In this book, Sandel does a good job in providing us with certain market transactions, analysing them, and then detailing the moral implications of said transactions.<br /><br />This book is divided into parts.  Below I will give the name of the part and an example of sections in that part that typifies a particular market transaction:<br /><br />(1) Jumping the Queue.  Sections in this part include (i) hired line standers (ii) ticket scalpers<br /><br />(2) Incentives.  Example sections: (i) paying kids for good grades (ii) paying to kill an endangered species (iii) cash for (female) sterilization<br /><br />(3) How Markets Crowd Out Morals.  Example sections: (i) hiring friends (ii) auctioning college admissions<br /><br />(4) Markets in Life and Death.  Examples include (i) betting on death (ii) death bonds (iii) Internet death pools<br /><br />(5) Naming Rights.  Examples: (i) ads in books (ii) commercials in classrooms (iii) bathroom advertising.<br /><br />Within each of these parts is at least one section on some aspect of economics.  Examples of these sections include:<br /><br />(1)  Markets versus Queues<br />(2)  The economic approach to life<br />(3)  Coercion and corruption<br />(4)  Ethics and economics<br /><br />The best thing about this book, in my opinion, is that it makes you think.  Some seemingly innocent business transactions are not so innocent at all when you think about them.<br /><br />When it comes to moral matters such as the moral limits of markets, we have to remember that we have heads to use, and we need to use them, for our own good and others'.  Nobody else can do our thinking for us and those who presume to do so should be viewed with suspicion.  Morality should provide the conditions on which we may all live the best lives we can.<br /><br />Finally, there are two problems I had with this book:<br /><br />First, I said above that each part has \\"at least one section on some aspect of economics\\"  I would've liked to have had all these particular sections in a separate part of their own.  By putting these sections throughout the book, it made the book seem scattered.<br /><br />Second, the subtitle of this book is \\"the moral limits of markets.\\"  Yet, there is no definition of the word \\"moral\\" in this book.!!  As well, I think a small, general discussion of morality (which is, I admit, a huge topic) at perhaps the beginning would have been beneficial.<br /><br />In conclusion, this book provides a good analysis of the morality behind some economic transactions.  I leave you with this book's final paragraph:<br /><br />\\"And so, in the end, the question of markets is really a question about how we want to live together,  Do we want a society where everything is up for sale?  Or are there certain moral and civic goods that markets do not honour and money cannot buy?\\"<br /><br />(first published 2012;  introduction;  5 chapters; main narrative 205 pages;  notes;  acknowledgements;  index;  a note about the author)<br /><br />&lt;&lt;Stephen PLETKO, London, Ontario, Canada>><br /><br />XXXXXr /><br />(first published 2012;  introduction;  5 chapters; main narrative 205 pages;  notes;  acknowledgements;  index;  a note about the author)<br /><br />&lt;&lt;Stephen PLETKO, London, Ontario, Canada>><br /><br />XXXXX

It was well sprinkled with interesting history questions, and I appreciated the section at the end that told the reader what was factual and what was fiction...very enlightening.  I think I would have liked it more if there was not such an overload of historical mysteries and it focused more tightly on a few and saved others for another book.
